Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">We report the effort in designing layered SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals decorated on nitrogen and sulfur dual-doped graphene aerogels (SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA) as anode material of SIBs. The optimized mass loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> along with the addition of nitrogen and sulfur on the surface of GAs results in enhanced electrochemical performance of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composite. In particular, the introduction of nitrogen and sulfur heteroatoms could provide more active sites and good accessibility for Na ions. Moreover, the incorporation of the stable SnS<sub>2</sub> crystal structure within the anode results in the superior discharge capacity of 527 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under a current density of 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup> upon 50 cycles. It maintains 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even the current density is increased to 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Aiming to further systematically study mechanism of composite with improved SIB performance, we construct the corresponding models based on experimental data and conduct first-principles calculations. The calculated results indicate the sulfur atoms doped in GAs show a strong bridging effect with the SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals, contributing to build robust architecture for electrode. Simultaneously, heteroatom dual doping of GAs shows the imperative function for improved electrical conductivity. Herein, first-principles calculations present a theoretical explanation for outstanding cycling properties of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composite.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D2637%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLinlin%2BFan%252C%2BXifei%2BLi%252C%2BXiaosheng%2BSong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D3%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b18195%26title%3DPromising%2BDual-Doped%2BGraphene%2BAerogel%252FSnS2%2BNanocrystal%2BBuilding%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D2648%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrical%20properties" class="article__tags__link">Electrical properties</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291619&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sulfur" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sulfur" class="article__tags__link">Sulfur</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sns2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SnS2">SnS<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Dual+Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_dual%20doping">dual doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene+Aerogel&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene%20aerogel">graphene aerogel</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cycling+Performance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cycling%20performance">cycling performance</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sustainable sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much attention in renewable energy and smart grid applications because of high sodium reserves and significantly lower cost as well as the insertion chemistry resembling lithium ion batteries (LIBs). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> It has been accepted that the Na ion intercalation into electrode materials is similar to that of Li ions. However, the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.06 Å) is about 1.4 times larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4, 5)</a> As a result, SIBs face significant challenges of anisotropic volume expansion, resulting in active material pulverization and insufficient contact with the current collector or conductive agent. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Additionally, from the energy density perspective, a considerable drawback is that the redox potential of Na is higher than that of Li. Therefore, the exploration of appropriate host anode materials with fast ion-diffusion pathways and large-sized tunnel structures is a primary bottleneck for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Over the past decade, graphite has been considered as a state-of-the-art anode material for LIBs. Unfortunately, it is an ineffective host for Na ions, showing the low theoretical capacity (&lt;35 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) because of larger Na ion radius. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 7 and 8">(7, 8)</a> Currently, significant efforts have been geared toward developing suitable materials for high-capacity SIB applications. Notably, many previous reports have demonstrated that tin and tin-based compounds can alloy with sodium and show good prospects as SIB anodes compared to carbon materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9-11)</a> For instance, SnSb@carbon nanocables anchored on graphene nanosheets were successfully designed, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> where simultaneous encapsulation of SnSb alloy and carbon coating better function to relieve the volume expansion/shrinkage. Another exceptionally high performing SIB anode was proposed to use crystalline SnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles confined in mesoporous carbon, which achieved superior SIB performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> In our group, the significant effects of SnO<sub>2</sub> crystallinity (including amorphous and crystalline) on electrochemical performance of SIBs were studied systemically for the first time. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Our work showed that the amorphous SnO<sub>2</sub> obtained enhanced cycling performance, and the Na ion diffusion coefficient was almost 2 times as high as crystalline SnO<sub>2</sub>. These inspiring phenomena clearly reveal that tin-containing compounds reveal great potential for use in SIBs. Unfortunately, tin–sodium alloys suffer from severe volume expansion (520% from Sn to Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn) during uptake or removal of sodium, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> which is even larger than that of the corresponding tin–lithium alloy, causing greater limitations for the realization of durable anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> SnS<sub>2</sub>, as a layered hexagonal CdI<sub>2</sub>-type crystal structure, consists of tin atoms sandwiched between two layers of closely packed sulfur slabs, with adjacent sulfur layers connected via van der Waals forces. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17-19)</a> SnS<sub>2</sub> can host Na ions and has a greater tolerance for cycling-induced volume changes due to the large interlayer spacing of 0.59 nm. As a result, this layered structure enables the convenient insertion and extraction of Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, the capacity fading is inevitably severe due to the low electrical conductivity of SnS<sub>2</sub> and dissolution of sulfur into the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Much efforts have been made so as to overcome this limitation. The ultrafine few-layered SnS<sub>2</sub> anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide was proposed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Exfoliated SnS<sub>2</sub> restacked on graphene was designed by the hydrolysis of lithiated SnS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Inconceivably, all of the above attempts show high capacity and ultralong cycle life.</div><div class="NLM_p">Motivated by the aforementioned considerations, graphene has been regarded as an ideal matrix due to the large theoretical surface area, high electronic conductivity, and excellent mechanical properties. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> More impressively, heteroatom (nitrogen, sulfur, and boron) doping in graphene could highly enhance its electrical conductivity as well as the surface hydrophilicity to facilitate electrode–electrolyte interactions and charge transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> For instance, 3D N-doped graphene foam can deliver charge/discharge capacities of 605.6/594.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> from 0.02 to 3.0 V after 150 cycles at 1 C in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Accordingly, great effort has been made to employ nitrogen doping in graphene to improve its properties as a matrix, such as in N-doped graphene/SnO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> N-doped graphene/NiO, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> and N-doped graphene/MoS<sub>2</sub> composites. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> However, studies on the function of sulfur doping in graphene-based composites are relatively limited. Remarkably, Wang et al. designed the nanocomposites with few-layered MoS<sub>2</sub> and sulfur-doped graphene via the facile solvothermal technique. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> The unique composite architecture derived from the “bridging effect” resulted in excellent electrochemical performance of the electrode. On the basis of the above discussion, it has been proposed that nitrogen and sulfur dual-doped graphene could carry more active sites and extrinsic defects, improving the electrochemical activity and conductivity simultaneously and contributing to energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Accordingly, our group synthesized nitrogen and sulfur dual-doped porous graphene aerogels. The results suggest that synergic effect of dual-doping heteroatoms into the graphene lattice is beneficial to enhanced performance, which facilitates the fast transfer for electrons and ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In the present study, we design layered SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals decorated on nitrogen and sulfur dual-doped graphene aerogels (SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA). When used as an anode material for SIBs, the SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are able to tolerate the volume changes that occur upon cycling while N,S-GA with large specific surface area can offer mass active sites to accommodate Na ions. More significantly, sulfur doping makes GAs more electron-rich which causes polarization between the surfaces of N,S-GA and SnS<sub>2</sub>, resulting in a strong electronic coupling between them (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>). Hence, the bridging layer of SnS<sub>2</sub> along with the sulfur atoms covalently bonded to the graphene aerogels guarantees the robust architecture of the anode materials. These results are also supported by density functional theory (DFT) calculations. In summary, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites with appropriate SnS<sub>2</sub> loading show improved electronic and ionic conductivity and enhanced sodium storage ability in terms of excellent cycling stability and high reversible capacity.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0009.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Illustration of (a) the Strong Interaction between SnS<sub>2</sub> and N,S-GA and (b) the Weak Interaction between SnS<sub>2</sub> and GA</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA Composites with Different SnS<sub>2</sub> Contents</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphite oxide (GO) was produced by the oxidative treatment of natural graphite via a modified Hummers’ method based on our previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33, 34)</a> SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites were synthesized using a facile hydrothermal method. Briefly, 65 mg of GO was dissolved in 45 mL of deionized water with sonication for 20 min. Three known amounts of SnO<sub>2</sub> sol were added to the suspension to control the SnS<sub>2</sub> loading in the composites, followed by 30 min vigorous stirring to form a homogeneous solution. 3.25 g of thiourea (Tianjin Jiangtian Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) was then mixed with above solution via another 30 min vigorous stirring. The resultant hybrid was transferred to a 50 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and heated in an oven at 180 °C for 24 h. The black products were washed several times with ethanol (Tianjin Jiangtian Chemical Technology Co., Ltd.) and deionized water by centrifugation at 10 000 rpm and subsequently freeze-dried. The collected samples were marked as SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III, respectively. The pristine graphene aerogels (GAs) were prepared via the same process without the addition of SnO<sub>2</sub> sol and thiourea. N,S-GAs were produced via the same process without the addition of SnO<sub>2</sub> sol. The synthesis process is further illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Scheme S1</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Physical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structures of nanocomposite were carried out via X-ray diffraction (XRD, DX-2700) using a Cu Kα (λ ≈ 1.54 Å) radiation source. The surface morphology of SnS<sub>2</sub> in the presence of N,S-GAs was collected via scanning electron microscopy (SEM, SU8010, Hitachi) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, FEI Tecnai G<sup>2</sup> F20). The equipped energy dispersive X-ray spectrometer (EDS) was used to analyze chemical composition. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Pyris Diamond6000 TG/DTA, PerkinElmer Co, America) was conducted to verify SnS<sub>2</sub> contents in composites. The elemental characteristics of composite materials were performed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, VG ESCALAB MK II). Raman spectra were tested on the LabRAM HR800 system.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical characterization was performed using CR2032 coin cells. The working electrode was synthesized by mixing composite material (70%), carbon black (20%), and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF, 10%) dispersed in 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP), then pasted on copper foil, and followed by drying under vacuum at 80 °C overnight. Then, the electrodes were punched into 12 mm disks in diameter, and the typical electrode loading was about 0.55 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Metallic sodium foil was used as the counter and reference electrodes. The electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC) (2:1 by volume) with a 10 vol % addition of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Afterward, test cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox (H<sub>2</sub>O, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1 ppm). The cells were galvanostatically charged and discharged by a multichannel battery testing system (LANHE CT2001A) between 0.01 and 3.0 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) at an alternating voltage of 5 mV over the frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz and cyclic voltammetry (CV) ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were recorded over an electrochemical workstation (Princeton Applied Research VersaSTAT4). All electrochemical characterizations were performed at room temperature.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The XRD phase of three SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA samples is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the pristine GAs exhibit two graphite-like diffraction peaks at (002) and (100), and the average layer number of GAs is 9 (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Meanwhile, the diffraction peaks that emerge in the three SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA samples show a similar crystalline structure, agreeing with those of standard XRD patterns of hexagonal SnS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card No. 23-0677). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Notably, SnS<sub>2</sub> in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III has greater crystallinity, indicated by the increased peak intensities compared to those of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Furthermore, the broad diffraction peaks seen in the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II patterns indicate the presence of smaller SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of (i) pristine GAs, (ii) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, (iii) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and (iv) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III. (b) TGA curves of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) is conducted to determine the mass loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> on the N,S-GA framework. TGA and DTG curves of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II show three distinct regions of weight loss (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). In the first region initial weight loss (7.2%) ranging from 25 to 250 °C is associated with the evaporation of physically adsorbed water. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> In the second region from 250 to 500 °C, weight loss (21.6%) may be owing to the oxidation of SnS<sub>2</sub> into SnO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The third region of weight loss (40.3%) between 500 and 800 °C is ascribed to the removal of N,S-GAs in the composite. Hence, the SnS<sub>2</sub> mass percentage is approximately 53% for SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II as seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. Accordingly, the SnS<sub>2</sub> mass percentages in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are 41% and 75%, respectively. Further structural insights into as-prepared samples are explored via Raman spectroscopy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The D-band (∼1336 cm<sup>–1</sup>) attributes to disorder and defects in the hexagonal graphitic layers, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> while the G-band (∼1574 cm<sup>–1</sup>) corresponds to the first-order scattering of the E<sub>2g</sub> mode of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The calculated <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratios for SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are 1.73, 1.71, and 1.64, respectively. Notably, compared with SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III exhibits a decreased <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio, implying an increase in the average size of the sp<sup>2</sup> graphitic domains. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The XPS test is employed to further validate element states of as-prepared SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, one can see the existence of tin, carbon, oxygen, sulfur, and nitrogen from the survey spectra. Clearly, the N 1s peak of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III is comparatively weaker, suggesting a lower amount of doped nitrogen. Furthermore, Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peaks are found in XPS analysis with an energy difference of 8.4 eV (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), confirming the presence of tin(IV). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> It can be seen from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c that the S 2p spectra of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II can be divided into two types of doublets. The first set of doublets consist of the peaks centered at 161.4 eV (S1) and 162.7 eV (S2) corresponding to binding energies of SnS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The second set of doublets are the higher-energy peaks seen at 163.8 eV (S3) and 164.9 eV (S4) that are assigned to the formation of a C–S–C bridge, indicating that sulfur is possibly covalently bonded with carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44, 45)</a> It is calculated that the total amount of sulfur doped in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II is 4.6%. In comparison, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III shows no obvious S3 and S4 peaks (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d) which illustrates that the amount of sulfur doped is insignificant. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f, the high-resolution N 1s peaks are able to deconvolute into three components: pyridinic-N (398.5 eV, N1), pyrrolic-N (400.1 eV, N2), and graphitic-N (401.3 eV, N3). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Moreover, as estimated by XPS results, the nitrogen concentrations of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are 2.41% and 1.88%, respectively. Note that the N1 and N2 species are dominant in the composites, suggesting that nitrogen heteroatoms mainly reside at the edges or at the nanoholes of the GAs. The vacancy and electron deficiency caused by nitrogen doping can provide a feasible pathway for Na ons, which is favorable for enhancing sodium transmission rates. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> Additionally, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figures 2">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h present the high-resolution C 1s spectra fitted with three types of carbon including C═C (284.6 eV, C1), C═N (285.4 eV, C2), and C–N (286.6 eV, C3). These results further support that the oxygen atoms in GAs have been substituted by nitrogen atoms successfully. Meanwhile, the overwhelming percentage of graphitic carbon is indicative of the graphitized nature of the GAs in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA. Overall, these analyses show that SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites have been successfully designed.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XPS analysis of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III: (a) the survey spectrum, (b) Sn 3d spectra; S 2p spectra: (c) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and (d) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III; N 1s spectra: (e) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and (f) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III; C 1s spectra: (g) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and (h) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are analyzed via SEM (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>). As observed, three samples exhibit a typical graphene aerogel network structure, and no obvious SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are observed in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II due to the small particle size. This contrasts with the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III sample where SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are observed to anchor onto the N,S-GAs because of the high mass loading and large SnS<sub>2</sub> particle size.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Typical SEM images of (a, b) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, (c, d) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and (e, f) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is important to note that an excess of SnS<sub>2</sub> particles can result in severe aggregation which may cause large volume changes upon cycling and loss of electrical contact with the N,S-GAs. In this regard, the samples are analyzed via TEM to exhibit distribution of SnS<sub>2</sub> on N,S-GAs, summarized in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. It can be seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b that low SnS<sub>2</sub> loading results in particles of approximately 5 nm decorating the N,S-GAs. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, suitable loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II shows an average particle size of 5 nm which uniformly anchors onto N,S-GAs. Additionally, the fringes with lattice spacings of 0.32 and 0.28 nm agree well with (100) and (101) planes in hexagonal SnS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Meanwhile, the diffraction rings of the selected area electron diffraction (SEAD) pattern (see insert of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c) can be indexed to (100), (101), and (102) crystal planes for SnS<sub>2</sub>. In comparison, TEM images of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f) indicate that the N,S-GAs are heavily embellished with 10 nm particles of SnS<sub>2</sub> due to the high SnS<sub>2</sub> loading of SnS<sub>2</sub>, and the aggregation of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals occurs severely. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a> presents the EDS spectrum of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, which reveals the presence of tin, sulfur, carbon, and nitrogen in the composites. The elemental mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) further illustrates the homogeneous distribution of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals in N,S-GAs.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. TEM and HRTEM images of (a, b) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, (c, d) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II (the inset is selected-area diffraction (SAED) pattern), and (e, f) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore sodium storage mechanisms of the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites, cyclic voltammetry is carried out on the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III electrode materials in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figures 5">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a–c. In the first cathodic scan, the peaks at higher voltage (1.20 and 1.62 V) can be associated with the formation of NaSnS<sub>2</sub>: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref49" aria-label="reference 23 and 49">(23, 49)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/am-2017-18195s_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>Note that the reduction peak at 1.20 V disappears at the second scan. It is assigned to the formation of irreversible solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film, which can be further confirmed by the cyclic voltammogram of pristine GAs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). Subsequently, the cathodic peaks from 0.65 to 0.30 V attributed to synthesis for metallic tin and sodium sulfide as well as the alloying process between tin and Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50, 51)</a> These reactions can be described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eqs 2">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>:<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/am-2017-18195s_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/am-2017-18195s_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Cyclic voltammetry curves of (a) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, (b) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and (c) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. The discharge/charge profiles of (d) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, (e) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and (f) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III in the 1st, 2nd, 10th, and 100th cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Intriguingly, these peaks are shifted to a higher potential at subsequent scans, suggesting that there is a phase transformation during the first cycle. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Furthermore, the corresponding oxidation peaks (0.25, 0.75, and 1.20 V) may be attributed to the desodiation reaction of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> However, the three above-mentioned peaks are inconspicuous in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II due to the imperfect crystallinity of SnS<sub>2</sub>, which can be confirmed by XRD results. Additionally, three higher voltage oxidation peaks (1.61, 1.84, and 2.10 V) can be found in all CV curves. The peak at 1.61 V is attributed to Na ion storage/release in GAs, the anodic peak concentrated in 1.84 V is assigned to the deinsertion process for NaSnS<sub>2</sub>, and the peak at 2.10 V corresponds to the sodium extraction from NaSnS<sub>2</sub> and oxidation of Sn to SnS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref54" aria-label="reference 23 and 54">(23, 54)</a> It is worth mentioning that although the CV curves of the three SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA are incomplete anastomosis due to the complex systems, all key points of the reaction mechanism are consistent with previous reports. The charge–discharge tests are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figures 5">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d–f. The initial discharge capacities of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are 1155.6, 1267.3, and 1567.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Meanwhile, the corresponding Coulombic efficiencies are 35.4%, 42.9%, and 43.4%, respectively. It can be observed that the high irreversible capacity arises for three samples. This phenomenon generally results from the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation due to the decomposition of electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 55 and 56">(55, 56)</a> During the subsequent cycles, it can be noticed that the specific capacity of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II decreases slower than that of both SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III, suggesting better cycling performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performances of bare SnS<sub>2</sub> (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> for detailed synthesis and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/suppl_file/am7b18195_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> for morphological and structural features), pristine GAs, N,S-GAs, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III are compared at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V from the second cycle (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The pristine GAs exhibit very poor specific capacity. In contrast, N,S-GAs obtain improved discharge capacity (177 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in 100th cycle) because of introduction of nitrogen and sulfur heteroatoms, which can facilitate the electrode–electrolyte interactions and charge transfer efficiently. Additionally, the cyclability of bare SnS<sub>2</sub> is significantly hampered due to the aggregation and pulverization issue. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Fortunately, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites all perform improved cycling properties. This phenomenon may be associated with the synergic effect of SnS<sub>2</sub> with N,S-GAs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> Furthermore, the sodium storage capacity of composites highly interrelates with SnS<sub>2</sub> content and heteroatom concentrations. SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I delivers the reversible capacity of 221.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> up to 100 cycles, which is lower than that of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III due to the low SnS<sub>2</sub> loading and inability to exploit the high specific surface area of GAs. SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III exhibits better sodium storage performance than SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II in the first 53 cycles, while when the cycling numbers increase to 100 cycles, the discharge capacity rapidly declines to about 288.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, showing poor cycling stability. Remarkably, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II achieves an excellent reversible capacity (360.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 100th cycle). Based on the above results, the increase of SnS<sub>2</sub> content in the composites enhances the reversible capacity. Unfortunately, the discharge capacity of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III rapidly fades, while the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II shows better cycling stability, demonstrating that both suitable mass loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> and the introduction of more nitrogenous and sulfurous groups to the surface of GAs facilitate capacity improvements. Meanwhile, the Coulombic efficiency of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II anode is more than 95% in the process of cycling.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Comparison of the cyclic performance of bare SnS<sub>2</sub>, pristine GAs, N,S-GAs, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> from the second cycle. (b) Comparison of the cyclic performance of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II at various current densities of 20, 50, 100, and 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate capability of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III at current densities: 50, 100, 200, 400, 800, and 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Capacity retention of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III at 50, 100, 200, 400, and 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the cycling performance of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II anode under varying current densities is tested and compared in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b. Fortunately, the discharge capacity remains 527 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles when the current density is 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The excellent Coulombic efficiency of approximately 100% from the second cycle is obtained. When tested under increasing current densities, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II exhibits similar cycling performance, showing insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> for composites have no effect on their cyclability, and the crystal structure remains relatively unchanged under the higher charge–discharge currents. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> Notably, the cycling performance is unstable due to the variable temperature in the testing environment. The rate capability of the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composite is further investigated, as presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c. In the case of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, a visible decrease of capacity is found with an increase in current density. For SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III, they deliver similar rate capacities when the electrodes are tested at low current densities (50, 100, and 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Nevertheless, discharge capacities of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III decline significantly compared to that of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II at high current densities of 400 and 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. More strikingly, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II maintains the capacity of 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a high current density of 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In the meantime, it recovers to a great extent, to around 434.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. This result is even better than the electrode cycling in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, implying excellent stability at high rate currents. Additionally, the relative capacity retention values based on the capacities obtained at various current densities are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d, which further demonstrates that SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II possesses superior rate capability in SIB performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic voltammograms of the SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III electrodes at varying scan rates are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figures 7">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b, respectively. It can be observed that the anodic potential shifts to higher voltages when the scan rate increases from 0.1 to 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. This behavior is caused by the increased polarization at higher sweep rates attributed to the kinetic limitations of sodium diffusion through the active materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref60 ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref60 ref61" aria-label="reference 60 and 61">(60, 61)</a> The following is the Randles–Sevcik equation: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62 ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62 ref63" aria-label="reference 62 and 63">(62, 63)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/am-2017-18195s_m004.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></span>In this equation, <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> represents peak current (A), <i>v</i> is the scan rate (V s<sup>–1</sup>), <i>n</i> is the number of reaction electrons (<i>n</i> = 1), <i>D</i> expresses Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), <i>A</i> indicates the electrode area (<i>A</i> = 1.13 cm<sup>2</sup>), and <i>C</i><sub>0</sub> is the concentration of Na ions in solution (<i>C</i><sub>0</sub> = 1 mol cm<sup>–3</sup>). According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>, the peak current is in direct proportion to square root of the scan rate (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c). Sodium diffusion coefficients of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III can be calculated from the slopes of these plots and are thus determined to be 3.80 × 10<sup>–12</sup> and 4.82 × 10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. These kinetics results imply that sodium storage performance is primarily related to the heteroatom doping, which can generate more extrinsic defects and active sites to accelerate Na ion diffusion. To further understand the electrode reaction kinetics for composites, EIS is carried out on the 100th cycle. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d, the Nyquist plots consist of the characteristic partially overlapped semicircles appearing at high and intermediate frequencies as well as a sloped line at low frequency. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref64 ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64 ref65" aria-label="reference 64 and 65">(64, 65)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e shows the equivalent circuit where <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> is ohmic electrolyte resistance, <i>R</i><sub>sei</sub> represents resistance of Na-ion migration through the SEI film, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is charge transfer resistance, CPE<sub>sei</sub> is passivation film capacitance, and CPE<sub>ct</sub> represents the double-layer capacitance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66 ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66 ref67" aria-label="reference 66 and 67">(66, 67)</a> It can be seen that SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II shows a much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> resistance in comparison with SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III, confirming that the electrode reaction kinetics and electron conduction of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II are improved. This phenomenon is primarily attributed to the increased nitrogenous and sulfurous groups on the surface of the GAs which significantly enhance the electrochemical performance of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref68'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. CV curves in the potential range of 0.01–3.0 V at scanning rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, and 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for (a) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II and (b) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III. (c) The corresponding relationship between <i>I</i><sub>p</sub> and <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. (d) The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-I, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-III in the 100th cycle. (e) The corresponding equivalent circuit used to simulate EIS curves.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Significantly, we carry out density functional theory (DFT) calculations to analyze the structural properties of the composite materials in order to better understand the improved cycling capability of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites with optimized SnS<sub>2</sub> mass loading. We design a (4 × 4) SnS<sub>2</sub> monolayer on a (6 × 6) graphene matrix. After stabilization, the lattice of the SnS<sub>2</sub> is evaluated to be 3.62, 3.62, and 5.85 Å at the <i>x-</i>, <i>y-</i>, and <i>z</i>-axes. The electronic structure of valence electrons can determine the electronic and physicochemical interaction between SnS<sub>2</sub> and GA, exhibiting an important influence on the structural stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref69'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref69" aria-label="reference 30 and 69">(30, 69)</a> The charge density changes of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA and SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figures 8">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b to further understand the interaction derived from the shared electron clouds. Intriguingly, massive charge transfer occurs from N,S-GA to SnS<sub>2</sub> in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA compared to SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA, which negatively polarizes the interface between SnS<sub>2</sub> and N,S-GA. Meanwhile, note that the sulfur atoms doped in the graphene aerogel show a strong bridging effect with SnS<sub>2</sub>. These results ensure robust composite architecture for SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figures 8">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>d illustrate the band structures of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA and SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA which have different characteristics. Some conduction bands of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA move downward toward the valence band, allowing a facile promotion of valence electrons across the band gap, resulting in enhanced electrical conductivity. In addition, there are broadened bands for SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA which leads to smaller electron effective mass, suggesting better electronic transport capacity under an external electric field. As seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figures 8">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>e and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>f, the calculated electronic density of states (DOS) reveals that the total DOS peak of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA is much higher than that of SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA, indicating a larger density of free electrons. Furthermore, after nitrogen and sulfur dual doping, the contribution of sulfur atoms for enlarging the total DOS peak becomes more remarkable, while the nitrogen atoms have no obvious effect due to the lower doped concentrations. Therefore, the heteroatom doping plays an imperative role in the improved electrical conductivity of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/medium/am-2017-18195s_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0008.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Charge density difference distributions for (a) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA and (b) SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA (the insets are the corresponding models). The band structures of (c) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA and (d) SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA. Calculated electronic density of states (DOS) of (e) SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA and (f) SnS<sub>2</sub>@GA.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b18195/asset/images/large/am-2017-18195s_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b18195&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, an effective technique is developed for successful synthesis of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites. As an anode material of SIBs, SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA-II with suitable mass loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> and nitrogenous/sulfurous groups in GAs presents outstanding cycling performance. It achieves high discharge capacity (527 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> upon 50 cycles) as well as superior rate capability (340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). Therefore, the introduced nitrogen and sulfur heteroatoms on the surface of GAs play an important role in SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA composites by providing more active sites and good accessibility for the Na ions. In addition, the optimized mass loading of SnS<sub>2</sub> results in the ideal synergic effect between SnS<sub>2</sub> and N,S-GAs. More significantly, the first-principles calculation confirms that the sulfur atoms doping in the GA shows a strong bridging effect with SnS<sub>2</sub>, which can ensure robust composite architecture. Simultaneously, the heteroatom dual doping of graphene plays an imperative role in the improved electrical conductivity of SnS<sub>2</sub>@N,S-GA. Altogether, this study may stimulate the search for improved anode materials for high energy SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div The carbonized leaf membrane possesses anisotropic surfaces and internal hierarchical porosity, exhibiting a high specific capacity of 360 mAh/g and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 74.8% as a binder-free, current-collector-free anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries.  Moreover, large-area carbon membranes with low contact resistance are fabricated by simply stacking and carbonizing leaves, a promising strategy toward large-scale sodium-ion battery developments.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqpldZ4FmRhnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPM1cMXMQbBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xis1Kmuw%253D%253D&amp;md5=24f3d46a90c1c53822a21989b6b9caab</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2017.02.200"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, T.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Study of Na<sub>0.66</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67–x</sub>Mo<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as Cathode Material for Sodium-ion Battery</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical study of Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.67-xMoxO2 as cathode material for sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jiale; Shen, Jianxing; Wang, Tailin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">709</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.67-xMoxO2 (x = 0, 0.03, 0.05, 0.07) were prepd. using a conventional solid state method and for the 1st time developed as promising cathode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The XRD patterns show that Mo6+ ions are successfully incorporated into the lattice of the Na-Ni-Mn-O system and the P2-type structure remains unchanged after substitution.  The introduction of Mo6+ in the Na-Ni-Mn-O system can improve capacity retention compared to the unsubstituted material during cycling.  An addnl. charge/discharge profile can be obsd. between 3.0 and 3.2 V for Mo-substituted samples, demonstrating that Na+/vacancy ordering can be suppressed during Na insertion/extn.  Na0.66Ni0.33Mn0.62Mo0.05O2 can deliver an initial capacity of 112 mA-h/g at 34 mA g-1 with a high av. voltage of 3.6 V and a capacity retention of 87% after 50 cycles.  EIS measurements demonstrate that Mo-substitution is an effective way to hinder the increase of inter-particle contact resistance by suppressing any possible irreversible phase transformation found at low Na contents.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNDmDAENJYIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPM1cMXMQbBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvFeisr0%253D&amp;md5=0a0c3d54f2a202da1fe5f2f4237b013c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201703086"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jin, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiao, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">1D Nanomaterials: Design, Synthesis, and Applications in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1703086</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have emerged in recent years, due to their advantages of natural abundance, low cost and environmental friendliness.  In this article, we review the up-to-date research progress on anode materials for Na-ion batteries from five respects: carbon-based materials, alloy-based materials, metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reaction, titanium-based compds. with insertion mechanism, and org. composites.  In particular, we not only summarize the Na-storage mechanism of these anodes, but also discuss the failure mechanism.  The problems and challenges assocd. with these anodes are pointed out.  Furthermore, on the basis of extensive reports and our exptl. studies, feasible strategies are suggested for designing high performance anode materials.  After further in-depth exploration and investigation, we believe that Na-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for low cost and large-scale energy storage systems in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrB4Pf2vZ5QrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljqYebEw7Oaxw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFelsrnN&amp;md5=8311789a2dc4e61dabff177245235c3e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.matlet.2015.05.041"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bao, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhong, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">One-pot Synthesis of FeF<sub>3</sub>/Graphene Composite for Sodium Secondary Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Mater.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Microstructural Evolution of Tin Nanoparticles during In Situ Sodium Insertion and Extraction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiang Wei; Liu, Xiao Hua; Mao, Scott X.; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The microstructural changes and phase transformations of tin nanoparticles during electrochem. sodiation were studied with a nanosized sodium ion battery using in situ TEM.  The first sodiation process occurred in two steps; i.e., the cryst. Sn nanoparticles were initially sodiated via a two-phase mechanism with a migrating phase boundary to form a Na-poor, amorphous NaxSn alloy (x ∼ 0.5), which was further sodiated to several Na-rich amorphous phases and finally to the crystd. Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75) via a single-phase mechanism.  The volumetric expansion was ∼60% in the first step and 420% after the second step.  However, despite the huge expansion, cracking or fracture was not obsd., which is attributed to the second step of the single-phase sodiation that accommodates large portion of the sodiation-induced stress over the entire particle.  Excellent cyclability was also obsd. during the reversible sodiation/desodiation cycles, showing great potential of Sn nanoparticles as a robust electrode material for rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4-_RnLjqf7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljqYebEw7Oaxw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFGqs7rO&amp;md5=3a9b283edadab9ac6dda7ef5c8bd18b7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.08.051"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Du, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">TiO<sub>2</sub>/Carbon Hollow Spheres as Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochim.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Flexible Paper-like Free-Standing Electrodes by Anchoring Ultrafine SnS2 Nanocrystals on Graphene Nanoribbons for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yang; Yang, Yongzhen; Wang, Xuzhen; Dong, Yanfeng; Tang, Yongchao; Yu, Zhengfa; Zhao, Zongbin; Qiu, Jieshan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15484-15491</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
